    </div>
    

    
</div>

                
                
                    <div class="c-article-section__content"><p>With a consistent global annual production of more than 100 million tons, as recorded between 1965 and 2014 (Food and Agriculture Organization of the United Nations)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="&#xA;                           Crops (FAO, accessed 1 August 2017); &#xA;                    http://www.fao.org/faostat/en/#data/QC&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d37286e615">1</a></sup>, the sweet potato, <i>I. batatas</i>, is an important source of calories, proteins, vitamins and minerals for humanity. It is the seventh most important crop in the world and the fourth most significant crop of China. In periods of shortages of basic cereal foods, <i>I. batatas</i> frequently served as the main food source for many Chinese people. It rescued millions of lives during and up to 3 years after the Great Chinese Famine in the 1960s and was subsequently raised as a main guarantor of food security in China.</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>Although the sweet potato is an outstanding crop, its genome has not yet been sequenced. The reason, at least in part, is that the genome has proved very difficult to assemble, being hexaploid (2<i>n</i>â=â6<i>x</i>â=â90) and highly polymorphic; with a base chromosome number of 15 and a genome size of about 4.4âGb, as estimated from its measured <i>C</i>-value (half the amount of DNA contained within a somatic cell of sweet potato)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Ozias-Akins, P. &amp; Jarret, R. L. Nuclear DNA content and ploidy levels in the genus Ipomoea. J. Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci. &#xA;                           119, 110â115 (1994)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d37286e637">2</a></sup>. Sweet potato has a composition of two B<sub>1</sub> and four B<sub>2</sub> component genomes (B<sub>1</sub>B<sub>1</sub>B<sub>2</sub>B<sub>2</sub>B<sub>2</sub>B<sub>2</sub>), as predicted by genetic linkage studies using random amplified polymorphic DNA and amplified fragment length polymorphism markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Ukoskit, K. &amp; Thompson, P. G. Autopolyploidy versus allopolyoloidy and low-density randomly amplified polymorphic DNA linkage maps of sweetpotato. J. Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci. &#xA;                           122, 822â828 (1997)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d37286e659">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Kriegner, A., Cervantes, J. C., Burg, K., Mwanga, R. O. M. &amp; Zhang, D. A genetic linkage map of sweetpotato (Ipomoea batatas (L.) Lam.) based on AFLP markers. Mol. Breeding &#xA;                           11, 169â185 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d37286e662">4</a></sup>. The degree of homology, however, could not be estimated with accuracy since its genomic components are still poorly characterized. Recently, the genome survey sequencing of <i>Ipomoea trifida</i>, the most probable diploid wild relative of <i>I. batatas</i>, has been reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Hirakawa, H. et al. Survey of genome sequences in a wild sweet potato, Ipomoea trifida (H. B. K.) G. Don. DNA Res. &#xA;                           22, 171â179 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d37286e672">5</a></sup>. Unfortunately, the current version of the <i>I. trifida</i> genome cannot serve as an adequate reference sequence for <i>I. batatas</i> because of its low N50 length statistics and even more because of the high abundance of gaps in the assembly, estimated at more than 30%. This example also points out the problems inherent in the usual circuitous tactics employed in polyploid genome sequencing projects, which always begin with simpler diploid relatives and assume no chromosomal structure variation between polyploidy and diploid wild relatives. For example, sequencing of the autotetraploid potato (<i>Solanum tuberosum</i>) employed a homozygous doubled-monoploid potato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="The Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature &#xA;                           475, 189â195 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d37286e686">6</a></sup>. Allotetraploid Upland cotton (<i>Gossypium hirsutumdi</i>, AADD)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of cultivated upland cotton (Gossypium hirsutum TM-1) provides insights into genome evolution. Nat. Biotechnol. &#xA;                           33, 524â530 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d37286e693">7</a></sup> began with diploid <i>Gossypium raimondii</i> (DD)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat. Genet. &#xA;                           44, 1098â1103 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d37286e701">8</a></sup> and <i>Gossypium arboreum</i> (AA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat. Genet. &#xA;                           46, 567â572 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d37286e708">9</a></sup>. Comparably, the sequencing of allohexaploid bread wheat (<i>Triticum aestivum</i>, AABBDD) was initiated by sequencing the genomes of <i>Triticum urartu</i> (AA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Ling, H. et al. Draft genome of the wheat A-genome progenitor Triticum urartu. Nature &#xA;                           496, 87â90 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d37286e718">10</a></sup> and <i>Aegilops tauschii</i> (DD)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Jia, J. et al. Aegilops tauschii draft genome sequence reveals a gene repertoire for wheat adaptation. Nature &#xA;                           496, 91â95 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d37286e726">11</a></sup> but is still struggling with the precise sequencing of isolated chromosome arms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="The International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium. A chromosome-based draft sequence of the hexaploid bread wheat (Triticum aestivum) genome. Science &#xA;                           345, 1251788 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d37286e730">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Choulet, F. et al. Structural and functional partitioning of bread wheat chromosome 3B. Science &#xA;                           345, 1249721 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d37286e733">13</a></sup>. All in all, a more cost-effective strategy and one that promises more efficient output for the direct sequencing of complex polyploid genomes is needed, especially for plant scientists, since polyploidy is a frequent and naturally occurring state in plants. Although the full genetic implications of polyploidization are still obscure, it is clear that this state provides an important pathway for plant evolution and specialization. For the plant genome investigator, however, decoding polyploidy genome remains a major problem.</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>More specifically, de novo assembly of polyploid genomes remains a critical unsolved technical problem. The high heterozygosity that comes from the presence of three or more copies of the monoploid genome will always hinder the genome assembly process, even with state-of-the-art assembly tools such as Platanus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Kajitani, R. et al. Efficient de novo assembly of highly heterozygous genomes from whole-genome shotgun short reads. Genome Res. &#xA;                           24, 1384â1395 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d37286e740">14</a></sup>, MaSuRCA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Zimin, A. V. et al. The MaSuRCA genome assembler. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           29, 2669â2677 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d37286e744">15</a></sup> and SOAPdenovo2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience &#xA;                           1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d37286e748">16</a></sup>. The reason being that genome assembly focuses on the vast majority of bases that are invariant across homologous chromosomes; since these invariant regions are intermittent along the whole chromosome, the result is fragmentation in polyploid assembly. To address this problem, one can separately sequence each chromosome using isolated chromosome arms as reported in the wheat genome project<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="The International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium. A chromosome-based draft sequence of the hexaploid bread wheat (Triticum aestivum) genome. Science &#xA;                           345, 1251788 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d37286e752">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Choulet, F. et al. Structural and functional partitioning of bread wheat chromosome 3B. Science &#xA;                           345, 1249721 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d37286e755">13</a></sup>. The chromosome isolation technique, however, is tedious and has a long way to go before it can become routine. Nevertheless, this strategy has inspired bioinformaticians to develop new tools for assembling each pair of homologous chromosomes separately. In contrast, with genome assembly, the haplotyping process pays more attention to DNA sequence differences among homologous chromosomes. The integration of genome assembly methods and haplotyping offers us a panoramic view of all the homologous chromosomes. Haplotyping itself, however, poses its own challenges. For example, the haplotyping of the human individual genome relies mainly on fosmid-based sequencing which is costly and time consuming<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Duitama, J. et al. Fosmid-based whole genome haplotyping of a HapMap trio child: evaluation of single individual haplotyping techniques. Nucleic Acids Res. &#xA;                           40, 2041â2053 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d37286e759">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Cao, H. et al. De novo assembly of a haplotype-resolved human genome. Nat. Biotechnol. &#xA;                           33, 617â622 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d37286e762">18</a></sup>. Since the distance of the adjacent variant positions between paternal and maternal chromosomes in humans is normally in the kilobase range<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="The International HapMap Consortium. A haplotype map of the human genome. Nature &#xA;                           437, 1299â1320 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d37286e767">19</a></sup>, it is beyond the capacity of current cost-effective sequencing platforms to cover at least two variant positions in most cases. Nevertheless, human haplotyping studies have already indicated that genome assembly and accurate haplotyping are tightly linked<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Cao, H. et al. De novo assembly of a haplotype-resolved human genome. Nat. Biotechnol. &#xA;                           33, 617â622 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d37286e771">18</a></sup>. Unfortunately, the computational phasing problem in polyploidy is considerably harder than for a diploid organism because in the polyploid case one cannot make inferences about the âotherâ haplotype once one has seen the first.</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>Our initial sequencing of the <i>I. batatas</i> genome revealed that the distance between adjacent polymorphic sites is roughly one-tenth of the distance in the human genome. Based on previous statistics, there are approximately 14 million polymorphic sites in the estimated 700~800âMb monoploid genome of <i>I. batatas</i>. This means that, on average, one read (100~150âbp length) from Illumina sequencing will cover two or three polymorphic sites. This density of such sites should permit phasing the <i>I. batatas</i> genome, employing cost-effective Illumina sequencing with paired-end libraries. The high heterozygosity of <i>I. batatas</i> makes genome assembly more challenging, but it simultaneously makes haplotyping easier.</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>Here we report the development of computational tools to derive a genomic sequence from a polyploid species and its genome that is phased to a large degree. The haplotype-resolved de novo assembly of the <i>I. batatas</i> genome was generated entirely based on Illumina sequencing data. The final ~836âMb assembly has a scaffold N50 of ~201âkb (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#Tab1">1</a>). The entire number of scaffolds was 35,919 and their lengths varied from 392 to 1,335,955âbp. Using gene and sequence synteny between <i>I. batatas</i> and <i>Ipomoea nil</i>, 75.7% of the current assembly has been anchored on 15 pseudochromosomes (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#Fig1">1</a>). In total, 49,063 genes with 78,781 gene models were extracted from the genome. Furthermore, this compressed monoploid genome was phased into six haplotypes in 644,810 regions. Using phylogenetic analysis of these haplotypes, a hypothesis on the origin of modern cultivated <i>I. batatas</i> could be proposed and examined. In addition, the availability of the haplotype-resolved genome allowed to trace back its hexaploidization process and permitted the estimation of the times of two recent whole genome duplication events; these were placed at approximately 0.8 and 0.5 million years ago.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary of assemblies</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                     <div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Outline of current sweet potato genome assembly."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Outline of current sweet potato genome assembly.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0002-z/MediaObjects/41477_2017_2_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0002-z/MediaObjects/41477_2017_2_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Summary of haplotyped percentage (red), single-nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) density (orange), frame-shifted events because of variants in haplotypes (yellow), sequencing coverage (green), repeat density with putative centromere positions (light blue), gene density (dark blue). Interchromosome duplicated genes are shown as links.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                  </div><section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec2">Sequencing data generation</h3><p>A newly bred carotenoid-rich cultivar of <i>I. batatas</i>, Taizhong6 (China national accession number 2013003), was used for genome sequencing (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#MOESM1">1</a>). During the genome survey stage, three sequencing libraries were constructed and sequenced on Hiseq2500 and GS FLXâ+âplatforms (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#MOESM2">1</a>, A500, A1kb and A454). After a preliminary genome assembly and read mapping for variant calling, the requirement for haplotyping of hexaploid <i>I. batatas</i> was estimated to be at least 40-fold monoploid genome coverage (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#MOESM1">2</a>). A new library was sequenced on the Nextseq500 platform to meet the estimated data requirement (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#MOESM2">1</a>, L500). Additional gel-free mate pair and 20k mate pair libraries were also sequenced to improve scaffolding (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#MOESM2">1</a>, MP and AMP). The insert size distributions of these paired-end libraries are shown in Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#MOESM1">3</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Initial assembly of consensus genome</h3><p>A heterozygosity-tolerant assembly pipeline, combining de Bruijn<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Peng, Y., Leung, H. C. M., Yiu, S. M. &amp; Chin, F. Y. L. IDBA-UD: a de novo assembler for single-cell and metagenomic sequencing data with highly uneven depth. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           28, 1420â1428 (2002)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d37286e1106">20</a></sup> and OLC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Margulies, M. et al. Genome sequencing in microfabricated high-density picolitre reactors. Nature &#xA;                           437, 376â380 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d37286e1110">21</a></sup> (overlap layout consensus) graph strategies, was employed to carry out the hexaploid genome assembly of <i>I. batatas</i>, using error corrected Illumina reads (see Methods). A total length of ~870âMb, mainly representing the monoploid genome, was assembled using this pipeline, with the largest scaffold being 3.7âMb (corresponding to a completely assembled endophyte <i>Bacillus pumilus</i> genome). The second largest scaffold, which harbours 54 genes, was 581âkb (and contained 133 contigs). The N50 of all scaffolds was ~60âkb with a 5,649âbp contig N50. The total number of scaffolds was 79,089 and their length varied between 312 and 3,723,026âbp. There were 3,796 scaffolds longer than 60âkb. Besides scaffolds, there were 991,314 contigs with a total length of ~436âMb. Among these, 92,790 contigs were longer than 1âkb harbouring a total of 175,679,534âbp. These contigs mainly reflected heterozygosity of the hexaploid genome since 97.35% contigs have been mapped back to scaffolds and one third of these contigs were found to match at full length, despite many single nucleotide mismatches or small indels, as shown in Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#MOESM1">4</a>.</p><p>We identified redundancy in the assembly based on exhaustive comparisons among scaffolds. When one scaffold was already covered by another longer scaffold with more than 85% sequence identity and more than 85% sequence length, the shorter one was removed. The largest scaffold in the preliminary assembly, the endophyte <i>Bacillus pumilus</i> genome, was also excluded for further analysis. After these procedures, there were 61,118 remaining scaffolds, of total length 822,598,598âbp, with 64,561âbp N50. Then these scaffolds were connected using 20âkb mate-pair library by Platanus. The N50 of all scaffolds after Platanus scaffolding was ~142âkb. The total number of scaffolds was 57,051 and their length varied between 392 and 1,152,062âbp. We call this version the âpreliminary assemblyâ.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Variant calling</h3><p>All the Illumina raw reads were mapped back to all scaffolds of the preliminary assembly (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#Tab1">1</a>). After removing the PCR duplicates, there were 1,725,677,696 mapped reads in the final bam file for variant calling using freebayes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Garrison, E. &amp; Marth, G. Haplotype-based variant detection from short-read sequencing. Preprint at &#xA;                    http://arxiv.org/abs/1207.3907v2&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d37286e1140">22</a></sup>. In total, there were 14,342,083 variations, consisting mainly of single nucleotide polymorphisms but also indels, across the 831,919,670âbp assembly. Most of the variant positions harboured two possible alleles (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#Fig2">2a</a>). We observed, on average, one polymorphic site every ~58âbp and a median distance of 20âbp between polymorphic sites. The distance distribution peaked at 6âbp and only 7% (1,004,917/14,285,053) of observed distances are longer than 150âbp (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#Fig2">2b</a>). These findings confirmed our earlier conclusion that the <i>I. batatas</i> genome is very heterozygous and formed the basis for phasing haplotypes using 100â150âbp Illumina reads. A high correlation (<i>r</i>â=â0.975) was found between number of variations and scaffold length (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#MOESM1">5</a>), which increases our chances for phasing the scaffolds.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Summary of variations."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Summary of variations.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0002-z/MediaObjects/41477_2017_2_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0002-z/MediaObjects/41477_2017_2_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>
                                       <b>a</b>, The total numbers of variations with different number of variant alleles. On average 831,919,670/14,342,083â=â58âbp with one variant, which means short reads (100âbp and 150âbp) are informative for haplotyping. Npos, number of positions; Nvar, number of variants. <b>b</b>, Adjacent variation distance distribution peaked at 6âbp (red dashed line). Only 1,004,917/14,285,053â=â7% observed distances are longer than 150âbp.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Phasing of haplotypes</h3><p>For phasing, we developed an algorithm that assigns reads in a seed region of polymorphic sites to six presumptive haplotypes. For example, three polymorphic sites with two alleles each would give rise to eight combinations. Sequencing errors, however, could artificially inflate the number of hypothesized haplotypes. Thus, we looked for six haplotypes that have the most support in terms of sequencing reads (see Methods and Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#Fig3">3</a>). Haplotypes can be identified from combinations of alleles over many polymorphic sites that are connected in a read. Our algorithm looks for combinations of two, three and four polymorphic sites. These phased regions were then extended continuously by searching for elongation of individual haplotypes. Finally, 542,361 regions were found and ~30% of the genome was phased into six haplotypes. These haplotypes could be further extended by connecting paired-end reads. Some of the paired-end reads map to haplotypes within one scaffold, and other paired-end reads connect haplotypes from different scaffolds because of better matching to the phased sequence. These connections are used for the haplotype-improved assembly.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Illustration of seed-finding algorithm."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Illustration of seed-finding algorithm.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0002-z/MediaObjects/41477_2017_2_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0002-z/MediaObjects/41477_2017_2_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Step 1, searching all possible seeds using different seed lengths. As an example, seed length 3 is shown here. Step 2, selecting the six highest supported sequence patterns in the seed; grey indicates less supported sequence patterns. Step 3, extension of particular seed based on supporting fragments. Reference, a sequence of variants in the scaffolds with each variant is coded as 0. Fragment, the sequence of variants from one chromosome which is covered by one read. Variants are coded 0â5. Paired-end information is not used here. Seed, a number of polymorphic sites together representing six or more unique sequence patterns. Haplotype, a certain region in one chromosome.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Haplotype-improved assembly</h3><p>All the Illumina raw reads were subsequently mapped against haplotype sequences generated by the phasing step. Only perfectly matched paired-end reads were considered as haplotype connections. The interscaffold and intrascaffold connections were separated for haplotype-based scaffolding and haplotype elongation, respectively. Gap sizes between connected scaffolds were estimated and super scaffolds were generated by SSPACE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C. V., Jansen, H. J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           27, 578â579 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d37286e1227">23</a></sup> resulting in haplotype-improved assembly. The final assembly N50 of this consensus genome was ~201âkb (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#Tab1">1</a>). The total number of scaffolds was 35,919 and lengths varied between 392 and 1,335,955âbp. When the contigs in the preliminary assembly were mapped against the haplotype-improved assembly, one-third of these contigs could be mapped back at full length though allowing for a fairly large number of mismatches. These contigs probably comprise several particular haplotypes (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#MOESM1">6</a>). Using a similar scenario for removing redundancy in scaffolds, there were 41,487 remaining contigs, with a total length of ~14âMb (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#Tab1">1</a>). There were 7,470 scaffolds in the haplotype-improved assembly with five or more genes, accounting for 75.7% of the total length of all scaffolds, which could be anchored to 15 pseudochromosomes according to the gene and sequence synteny between our assembly and the newly released genome of <i>I. nil</i>
                           <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Hoshino, A., Jayakumar, V., Nitasaka, E., Toyoda, A., Noguchi, H., Itoh, T., Shin-I, T., Minakuchi, Y., Koda, Y. &amp; Nagano, A. J. et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the Japanese morning glory Ipomoea nil. Nat. Commun. &#xA;                           7, 13295 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d37286e1244">24</a></sup>. The 15 pseudochromosomes with the remaining scaffolds served as our final assembly, which we annotated, used for final phasing of haplotypes, and from which we traced the hexaploidization of the <i>I. batatas</i> genome (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#Fig1">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genome annotation and cDNA validation</h3><p>After six transcriptome data sets were mapped to the final assembly, 78,781 gene models from 49,063 gene loci were extracted and revised by StringTie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Pertea, M. et al. StringTie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from RNA-seq reads. Nat. Biotechnol. &#xA;                           33, 290â295 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d37286e1262">25</a></sup> and TACO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Niknafs, Y. S., Pandian, B., Iyer, H. K., Chinnaiyan, A. M. &amp; Iyer, M. K. TACO produces robust multisample transcriptome assemblies from RNA-seq. Nat. MethodsÂ 14, 68â70 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d37286e1266">26</a></sup>, respectively. To validate the predicted gene models from transcriptome data, 10,063 expressed sequence tags (ESTs) were generated by Sanger sequencing. These sequences were then mapped to the genome. Of these, 9,425 were located correctly and found to have corresponding predicted gene models. The functional annotations of predicted genes were obtained by homologous protein sequence searching in the public databases Uniprot (<a href="http://www.uniprot.org/">http://www.uniprot.org/</a>) and NCBI protein database (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/</a>). RepeatModeler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Smit, A. &amp; Hubley, R. RepeatModeler - 1.0.8 (Institute for Systems Biology, 2015); &#xA;                    https://sourceforge.net/u/djinnome/jamg/ci/47152a01077445af52726d76270e60bb360bb2f2/tree/3rd_party/RepeatModeler-open-1.0.8/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d37286e1284">27</a></sup> was employed to identify repeat sequences in the present genome. The repeat sequence classifications are summarized in TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#Tab2">2</a>. The horizontally transferred T-DNAs reported by Kyndt et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Kyndt, T. et al. The genome of cultivated sweet potato contains Agrobacterium T-DNAs with expressed genes: an example of a naturally transgenic food crop. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA &#xA;                           112(18), 5844â5849 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d37286e1292">28</a></sup> were also investigated in the current genome. We found multiple copies of these T-DNAs suggesting that the horizontal gene transfer event has happened before hexaploidization of <i>I. batatas</i> (T-DNA1, NCBI accession KM052616, hit scaffold14997; and T-DNA2, NCBI accession KM052617 have been located in pusedochromosome 7).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Summary of repeat sequence identification</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Gene cluster identification</h3><p>The discovery of operon-like gene clusters in plant genomes has prompted attempts to decode the regulatory mechanism in plant specialized compound biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="NÃ¼tzmann, H. W. &amp; Osbourn, A. Regulation of metabolic gene clusters in Arabidopsis thaliana. New Phytol. &#xA;                           205, 503â510 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d37286e1663">29</a></sup>. Gene clusters of paralogous genes have been identified in a wide range of species including maize, lotus, cassava, sorghum, poppy, tomato, potato, rice, oat and <i>Arabidopsis</i>
                           <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Fernie, A. R. &amp; Tohge, T. Location, location, location â no more! The unravelling of chromatin remodeling regulatory aspects of plant metabolic gene clusters. New Phytol. &#xA;                           205, 458â460 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d37286e1670">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Boycheva, S., Daviet, L., Wolfender, J. L. &amp; Fitzpatrick, T. B. The rise of operon-like gene clusters in plants. Trends Plant Sci. &#xA;                           19, 447â459 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d37286e1673">31</a></sup>. In the <i>I. batatas</i> genome, we identify four clusters of six or seven genes for alkaloids (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#Fig4">4</a>, GC002, GC006, GC014 and GC024), three six-to-eight gene clusters for terpenes (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#Fig4">4</a>, GC001, GC005 and GC031), and a six-gene cluster for cellulose (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#Fig4">4</a>, GC028) by searching orthologous genes using Exonerate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Slater, G. S. &amp; Birney, E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC Bioinformatics &#xA;                           6, 31 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d37286e1690">32</a></sup>. In addition to the gene clusters shown in Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#Fig4">4</a>, there were 68 more gene clusters found in the current genome assembly (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#MOESM3">2</a>). The results indicate that pathway regulation via clustered genes is commonly used in the genus <i>Ipomoea</i>. Although all the orthologous genes found here are based on protein sequence similarity, their biological functions are not necessarily the same as those reported for similar gene clusters in other species. Nevertheless, the identified gene clusters in <i>I. batatas</i> open up possibilities for investigating metabolic regulatory mechanisms in this plant.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Identified gene clusters in present I. batatas genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Identified gene clusters in present <i>I. batatas</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0002-z/MediaObjects/41477_2017_2_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0002-z/MediaObjects/41477_2017_2_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Blue bars indicate the gene structure, the exon as box and the intron as line. The red boxes indicate genes from a cluster and the grey boxes indicate unrelated genes. The best hit genes of GC001 are <i>ZmBx4</i>, <i>AtMRO</i>, <i>AtMRO</i>, <i>PsCYP82â</i>Ã<i>â2</i>, <i>SbCYP71E</i>, <i>AtMRO</i>, <i>SlP450-1</i> and <i>SbCYP71E</i>; the best hit gene of GC002 is <i>SlGAME7</i>; the best hit genes for GC005 are <i>ZmBx5</i>, <i>PsCYP82Y1</i>, <i>OsCYP76M5</i>, <i>LjCYP71D11</i>, <i>PsCYP82â</i>Ã<i>â2</i> and <i>LjCYP736A2</i>; the best hit genes for GC006 are <i>PsCYP82â</i>Ã<i>â1</i>, <i>PsCYP82â</i>Ã<i>â2</i>, <i>OsCYP71Z6</i>, <i>ZmBx4</i>, <i>LjCYP79D4</i> and <i>PsCYP82</i>âÃâ<i>2</i>; the best hit genes for GC014 are <i>PsCXE1</i>, <i>PsCXE1</i>, <i>PsCXE1</i>, <i>SlGAME3</i>, <i>StSGT1</i> and <i>MeUGT85K4</i>; the best hit genes for GC024 are <i>SlGAME11</i>, <i>SlGAME7</i>, <i>SlGAME7</i>, <i>SlGAME3</i>, <i>SlGAME3</i> and <i>SlGAME3</i>; the best hit gene of GC028 is <i>StCeSy</i>; the best hit genes for GC031 are <i>MeCYP71E</i>, <i>OsCYP71Z7</i>, <i>OsCYP99A2</i>, <i>SlP450-1</i>, <i>OsCYP71Z6</i> and <i>SlP450-1</i>. All the detailed gene cluster information can be found in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#MOESM3">2</a>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Updating and validation of haplotypes</h3><p>Based on the final assembly, we applied our phasing procedure a second time and phased 644,810 regions into 1,928,359,400âbp haplotype sequences. Thus, the new assembly leads to extended haplotypes in 18.9% more regions and a 29.5% improvement of total length. We validated these final haplotypes using a set of Roche 454 reads (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#MOESM2">1</a>, A454) that had been produced earlier but which had not been utilized for assembly or phasing. The reads are on the order of 1000âbp long and can thus serve to identify errors in the haplotype reconstruction. A large fraction of these 454 reads display haplotypes that have been correctly reconstructed by our short-read based methodology. More than 60% of overlaps between haplotypes and 454 reads are identical at variant loci. The longest reconstructed haplotype contained 92 polymorphic sites without any mismatch indicated by 454 reads. There may be many longer perfectly reconstructed haplotypes that remain undetected because of the limited 454 read length (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#MOESM1">7</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Variant updating in phased regions</h3><p>Variants from freebayes have been updated according to phased haplotypes. When all six haplotypes covered the specific variant position, our method distinguishes two possible situations: (1) the six haplotypes indicate the same variant, and (2) some haplotypes indicate different variants. Situation (1) indicates an error in variant calling and we remove the variant position from the original VCF file. For situation (2), our method ranks alleles based on the number of supporting haplotypes. Then the first rank is picked as the reference allele and the rests are considered as the alternative ones. Using this strategy, we removed 115,228 variant positions and updated 1,514,176 variant positions to generate the final VCF file.</p><p>The phased haplotypes in protein coding region also allowed us to evaluate the impact of variants. We focused on the frame shift events, identifying 172,830 events across 34,691 putative transcripts (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#Fig1">1</a>, yellow circle). Furthermore, when all haplotype sequences were mapped back to the diploid <i>I. trifida</i> consensus genome, we could identify the conserved ancient variants and haplotypes that are shared between this hexaploid organism and its diploid pregenitors. This also led us to check the phylogenetic relationship of phased haplotypes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Phylogenetic analysis of homologous chromosome regions</h3><p>Phylogenetic analysis was applied to the final haplotype-resolved genome. All the regions phased into six haplotypes were used for constructing UPGMA (unweighted pair group method with arithmetic mean) trees with the MEGA-Computing Core<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Kumar, S., Stecher, G., Peterson, D. &amp; Tamura, K. MEGA-CC: computing core of molecular evolutionary genetics analysis program for automated and iterative data analysis. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           28, 2685â2686 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d37286e1916">33</a></sup>. We generated 644,360 phylogenetic trees and determined the distribution of their topologies over the six possibilities, explaining the evolution of six haplotypes. Note that between any two such trees we do not identify the sequences, but rather focus on the branching pattern alone. We denote the topologies as annotated in Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#MOESM1">8</a>. The branching pattern of two haplotypes versus four haplotypes is the stable majority class with or without consideration of repetitive sequences. Furthermore, among the four-haplotype subgroup, the division two versus two dominates (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#MOESM1">8</a>). The average branch length of these 2:4â2:2 trees were obtained and a consensus tree was constructed (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#Fig5">5a</a>). These results suggest two whole-genome duplication (WGD) events in <i>I. batatas</i> hexaploidization history together with a further diversification of the B<sub>2</sub> subgenome (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#Fig5">5b</a>). Assuming a 0.7% mutation rate (base pairs per million years<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Ossowski, S. et al. The rate and molecular spectrum of spontaneous mutations in Arabidopsis thaliana. Science &#xA;                           327, 92â94 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d37286e1938">34</a></sup>) the tetraploid progenitor of <i>I. batatas</i> was produced by the first WGD event estimated at 0.8 million years ago. The origin of modern cultivated <i>I. batatas</i> would then have been the result of an initial crossing between this tetraploid progenitor and a diploid progenitor, followed by the second WGD event occurring about 0.5 million years ago. The most probable diploid progenitor of <i>I. batatas</i> is also the most likely ancestor of modern <i>Ipomoea trifida</i>, although the tetraploid progenitor is still unknown. It might be identified, however, by a genome survey of the genomes of modern tetraploid species in <i>Ipomoea</i> genus.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Evolutionary history of cultivated I. batatas revealed by phylogenetic analysis of homologous chromosome regions."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Evolutionary history of cultivated <i>I. batatas</i> revealed by phylogenetic analysis of homologous chromosome regions.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0002-z/MediaObjects/41477_2017_2_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0002-z/MediaObjects/41477_2017_2_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>
                                       <b>a</b>, The dominant topology structure of all phylogenetic trees. Numbers indicate the average branch length of trees in this structure. <b>b</b>, The time points of B<sub>2</sub> subgenome specialization and two whole-genome duplication events were estimated as 1.3, 0.8 and 0.5 million years ago (Ma). The estimation is based on 0.7% mutation rate per million years. The dashed curve indicates the crossing between diploid and tetraploid progenitors.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><p>Here, we have reported the generation of sequencing data and the development of the necessary algorithms to compute long haplotype segments for the <i>I. batatas</i> genome. Based on an initial assembly and phasing, the computational process was repeated, yielding an improved assembly. In principle, haplotype phasing and haplotype-aided assembly can be iterated further to improve both results. In the present work, we only updated haplotypes based on the final assembly and observed remarkable improvements in terms of more phased regions and longer haplotype length. Paired-end reads have been employed to extend haplotypes both within and across assembly scaffolds. Overall, high-quality assembly and phasing can now be achieved with a comparatively low investment in sequencing (two runs on a HiSeq2500, two runs on a NextSeq500 and one run on a Hiseq4000; see Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#MOESM2">1</a>) but increased computational effort, both in terms of algorithm development and sheer computational power.</p><p>Based on the gene synteny between the haplotype-improved assembly and the <i>I. nil</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Hoshino, A., Jayakumar, V., Nitasaka, E., Toyoda, A., Noguchi, H., Itoh, T., Shin-I, T., Minakuchi, Y., Koda, Y. &amp; Nagano, A. J. et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the Japanese morning glory Ipomoea nil. Nat. Commun. &#xA;                           7, 13295 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d37286e2012">24</a></sup>, we generated 15 pseudochromosomes of <i>I. batatas</i>. Although the precise reconstruction of entire chromosomes is not possible using this methodology and is reserved for alternative approaches including long-read sequencing technologies, many evolutionary questions regarding the ancestry of polyploid organisms can now be tackled using the methodologies we have developed. For the <i>I. batatas</i> genome, we have shown, based on the available haplotype alignments, that the majority of computed phylogenetic trees groups two haplotypes versus four, the latter being in turn symmetrically grouped into two and two. The presence of the other, less supported phylogenetic groupings may indicate a high recombination rate between B<sub>1</sub> and B<sub>2</sub> subgenomes. Although the different haplotype alignments obtained by our data and analysis do not allow identification of the full set of chromosomal connections among the segments, this phylogenetic pattern dominates. It is in agreement with a scenario in which modern cultivated <i>I. batatas</i> originated from a cross between a diploid progenitor and a tetraploid progenitor, followed by a whole-genome duplication event. The precise timing of these evolutionary events, however, relies on an estimation of the average mutation rate which remains somewhat uncertain. Nevertheless, we have demonstrated the power of phylogenetic analysis of the haplotypes derived by our approach in the reconstruction of the hexaploidization history of <i>I. batatas</i>. When applied to the polyploidization of plants in general, phylogenetic analysis based on haplotype reconstruction could prove to be the most reliable way to study the origin of each set of chromosomes in complex polyploid organisms.</p><p>Our seed-based computational approach has thus proven successful on this very heterozygous genome, even with only short sequencing reads. This half haplotype-resolved hexaploid genome represents the first successful attempt to investigate the complexity of chromosome sequence composition directly in a polyploid genome using sequencing of the polyploid organism itself rather than any of its simplified proxy relatives. Adaptation and application of our approach should provide higher resolution in future genomic structure investigations, especially for similarly complex genomes. The approach presented here has a high degree of flexibility, which can be employed with many kinds of sequencing technologies and is ready to use for haplotyping tasks in a wide range of research programmes. With the availability of longer reads in the future, the same computational philosophy should be applicable for phasing of genome segments in other polyploid organisms, even when the density of polymorphic sites is lower. Given the importance of <i>I. batatas</i> as a crop species, a resolved genome will be of prime importance for improving yield security and nutritional quality via gene or genome engineering. Such avenues are now open for, on the one hand, rendering the plant more stress tolerant and disease resistant, classical goals of plant breeding, but, on the other hand, for better understanding the metabolic capacities of this species in allowing effective biofortification as has recently been demonstrated in cassava<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Li, K. T. et al. Increased bioavailable vitamin B6 in field-grown transgenic cassava for dietary sufficiency. Nat. Biotechnol. &#xA;                           33, 1029â1032 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d37286e2039">35</a></sup>. We believe, therefore, that the methodology presented in this paper additionally presents an important advance in the genomic analysis of polyploid organisms. Meanwhile, the haplotype-resolved genome sequence of sweet potato is the beginning of the precise genome manipulation era for this untapped human food resource.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec13" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec13-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec13">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec13-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>A newly bred carotenoid rich cultivar of sweet potato (<i>I. batatas</i>), Taizhong6, whose China national accession number is 2013003, was selected as the target cultivar. Total genomic DNA was isolated from in vitro cultured plants following the method described by Kim and Hamada<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Kim, S. H. &amp; Hamada, T. Rapid and reliable method of extracting DNA and RNA from sweetpotato, Ipomoea batatas (L). Lam. Biotechnol. Lett. &#xA;                           27, 1841â1845 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d37286e2058">36</a></sup>. In total, six sequencing libraries were constructed and sequenced on Hiseq2500, Nextseq500, Hiseq4000 and GS FLXâ+âplatforms (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#MOESM2">1</a>, A500, A1kb, L500, MP, AMP and A454) according to the manufacturerâs instructions (Illumina, Inc. and Roche Applied Science), respectively.</p><p>The main steps of consensus genome assembly were as follows:</p><ol class="u-list-style-none">
                    <li>
                      <span class="u-custom-list-number">a)</span>
                      
                        <p>Read-correction of all Illumina data using the BFC package (<a href="https://github.com/lh3/bfc">https://github.com/lh3/bfc</a>).</p>
                      
                    </li>
                    <li>
                      <span class="u-custom-list-number">b)</span>
                      
                        <p>Assembling all short reads by IDBA-UD with âmink 100 âmaxk 151 âstep 23.</p>
                      
                    </li>
                    <li>
                      <span class="u-custom-list-number">c)</span>
                      
                        <p>Further assembling the IDBA outputs by a long read assembler (Newbler 3.0).</p>
                      
                    </li>
                    <li>
                      <span class="u-custom-list-number">d)</span>
                      
                        <p>Two scaffolding runs using the Platanus scaffolder on Newbler 3.0 output.</p>
                      
                    </li>
                    <li>
                      <span class="u-custom-list-number">e)</span>
                      
                        <p>Gap closing using all corrected Illumina reads (Platanus GapCloser).</p>
                      
                    </li>
                    <li>
                      <span class="u-custom-list-number">f)</span>
                      
                        <p>Remove redundancy in preliminary assembly scaffolds.</p>
                      
                    </li>
                    <li>
                      <span class="u-custom-list-number">g)</span>
                      
                        <p>Platanus scaffolding using 20âkb insertion library.</p>
                      
                    </li>
                  </ol>
                        <p>Detailed information on the pipeline presented in this paper can be found in the Supplementary Information.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">Haplotyping algorithm and pipeline</h3><p>All the Illumina raw reads were mapped back to all scaffolds using BWA (Version 0.7.12-r1039). PCR amplification duplicates were removed via Samtools (version 0.1.19-44428âcd). Freebayes (version 0.9.14-19-g8a407cf) was employed for variant calling in the hexaploid genome.</p><p>For haplotyping, our method takes FASTA, BAM and VCF formatted files as inputs. We based the reconstruction on seed regions, which are small sets of polymorphic sites. For example, three polymorphic sites with two alleles each would allow up to 2âÃâ2âÃâ2â=â8 haplotypes. Only a subset of those will be supported by reads. The algorithm searches for all possible seed regions containing six or more sequence patterns. Seeds, however, can be interleaved. Haplotypes in a seed region are sorted by the number of their supporting reads. Different seed regions are sorted according to the number of supporting reads for the sixth-most strongly supported haplotype, because we expect six haplotypes.</p><p>The following steps were done iteratively on each seed region from the sorted seed list. The six most supported sequence patterns in each seed region were considered to be the six haplotype cores. Then, each haplotype was built up on each core according to the supporting reads for that sequence pattern (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#Fig3">3</a>). The haplotypes were extended via uniquely matched reads. A uniquely matched read is a read that matches exactly one of the six haplotypes while being distinct from the other haplotypes in the seed region. Chaining haplotypes through uniquely matched reads produced six haplotypes in an extended seed region (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#MOESM1">9a,b</a>).</p><p>Then the adjacent overlapping seed regions were merged (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#MOESM1">9c</a>). One haplotype in a seed region was merged to a haplotype in the adjacent seed region if they shared a uniquely matching read with each other. Thereafter, we utilized paired-end reads to connect the haplotypes obtained so far. All the Illumina raw reads were mapped back to the phased haplotypes. Only perfectly matched paired-end reads were considered as haplotype connections. The innerscaffold haplotyping exploited the paired-end reads within one scaffold. The algorithm started with the highest supported connection and merged it as a new haplotype. Possible conflicts were checked and avoided in this step. A conflict occurred when two haplotypes in one seed region were connected via a path through the haplotypes in other seed regions. Paired-end reads were further utilized for interscaffold connection to elongate and merge the haplotype from different scaffolds. The haplotyping method utilizes parallel computation in order to speed up the analysis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">Haplotype evaluation using 454 reads</h3><p>To evaluate the haplotyping accuracy, we used a set of 454 (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#MOESM2">1</a>, A454) reads that had been produced earlier but were not utilized for assembly or phasing. Each 454 read can be considered as a short DNA fragment from one chromosome, except for some chimeric reads in rare cases. Roche 454-trimmed reads were mapped against genome assembly. Only the polymorphic sites indicated by variant calling were extracted and their overlaps with haplotypes were evaluated. The âmatchâ and âmismatchâ sites of each overlap were recorded for further analysis and visualization.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">Haplotype-improved assembly and variant correction</h3><p>A python script was employed to convert all interscaffold connection information into SSPACE tab format. Exhaustive comparisons among scaffolds using blast were employed to identify and remove redundancy in scaffolds. If one scaffold was already covered by another longer scaffold with more than 85% sequence identity and more than 85% sequence overlap, the shorter one was removed. Additional manual checking of long candidates (&gt;10âkb) via Circos visualization was also included. Finally, the non-redundant scaffold sequences without the endophyte <i>Bacillus pumilus</i> genome were employed as input for scaffolding.</p><p>We refined variants according to phased haplotypes. There are three possibilities for one phased variant: (1) all six haplotypes covered the variant, (2) some of the haplotypes covered the variant and (3) the variant was located outside of haplotype blocks. There was insufficient information to update alleles in the second and third categories. Our method distinguished the first group from the other two and categorizes its members in the following subgroups: (1) all alleles are the same and (2) the alleles are different. The first subgroup was put aside as an error in variant calling, which meant that there was insufficient support to consider this position as a variant position. We therefore removed the variant calling result of this position from the original VCF file. For the second group, our method ranked alleles firstly based on the number of supporting haplotypes, secondly according to the number of supporting reads. Then the first allele was picked as a reference allele and the rest were considered as alternative alleles.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec18">Genome annotation</h3><p>Six transcriptome data sets from four previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Firon, N. et al. Transcriptional profiling of sweetpotato (Ipomoea batatas) roots indicates down-regulation of lignin biosynthesis and up-regulation of starch biosynthesis at an early stage of storage root formation. BMC Genomics &#xA;                           14, 460 (2013)." href="#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d37286e2219">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang, Z. et al. De novo assembly and characterization of root transcriptome using Illumina paired-end sequencing and development of cSSR markers in sweetpotato (Ipomoea batatas). BMC Genomics &#xA;                           11, 726 (2010)." href="#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d37286e2219_1">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Xie, F. et al. De novo sequencing and a comprehensive analysis of purple sweet potato (Ipomoea batatas L.) transcriptome. Planta &#xA;                           236, 101â113 (2012)." href="#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d37286e2219_2">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Tao, X. et al. Digital gene expression analysis based on integrated de novo transcriptome assembly of sweet potato [Ipomoea batatas (L.) Lam.]. PLoS ONE &#xA;                           7, e36234 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d37286e2222">40</a></sup> and two additional RNA-seq experiments were mapped on the current genome using HISAT2 and HISAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Kim, D., Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. HISAT: a fast spliced aligner with low memory requirements. Nat. MethodsÂ 12, 357â360 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d37286e2226">41</a></sup>. These transcriptome data represented different plant tissues such as leaves, petioles, stems and roots from different developmental stages. All gene models were extracted by StringTie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Pertea, M. et al. StringTie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from RNA-seq reads. Nat. Biotechnol. &#xA;                           33, 290â295 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d37286e2230">25</a></sup>. A consensus transcriptome was generated by TACO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Niknafs, Y. S., Pandian, B., Iyer, H. K., Chinnaiyan, A. M. &amp; Iyer, M. K. TACO produces robust multisample transcriptome assemblies from RNA-seq. Nat. MethodsÂ 14, 68â70 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d37286e2234">26</a></sup>. The obtained transcripts were further annotated using homologous protein searching in the public databases NCBI and Uniprot.</p><p>RepeatMasker (Version open-4.0.5) was used to mask and classify repeat sequences in the genome. RepeatModeler (Version 1.0.8)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Smit, A. &amp; Hubley, R. RepeatModeler - 1.0.8 (Institute for Systems Biology, 2015); &#xA;                    https://sourceforge.net/u/djinnome/jamg/ci/47152a01077445af52726d76270e60bb360bb2f2/tree/3rd_party/RepeatModeler-open-1.0.8/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d37286e2241">27</a></sup> was employed to identify novel repeat sequences.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec19">Phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>A python script was developed to extract alignments from all phased regions. MEGA-Computing Core<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Kumar, S., Stecher, G., Peterson, D. &amp; Tamura, K. MEGA-CC: computing core of molecular evolutionary genetics analysis program for automated and iterative data analysis. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           28, 2685â2686 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0002-z#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d37286e2253">33</a></sup> was utilized to compute all the phylogenetic trees of extracted alignments on a computer farm. Trees were classified into groups based on their topology structures and average haplotype lengths in these groups were compared. The dominant tree structure was selected and a consensus tree with average branch lengths was obtained.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec20">Data and code accessibility</h3><p>The <i>I. batatas</i> genome sequence, including pseudochromosomes and unanchored scaffolds, haplotype-resolved regions, and unplaced contigs, are publicly available at the sweet potato genome browser <a href="http://public-genomes-ngs.molgen.mpg.de/SweetPotato/">http://public-genomes-ngs.molgen.mpg.de/SweetPotato/</a> and Ipomoea Genome Hub <a href="http://www.ipomoea-genome.org/">http://www.ipomoea-genome.org/</a>. Two transcriptome data sets and one sequenced cDNA library are also available for download. cDNA clone delivery is also possible upon request. The assemblies and the WGS raw data have been deposited with European Nucleotide Archive (ENA) under project number PRJEB14638 and National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) under project number PRJNA301667. The Ranbow program, the implementation of the described algorithm, is available from <a href="https://www.molgen.mpg.de/ranbow">https://www.molgen.mpg.de/ranbow</a>.</p></div></div></section>

                  QTL analysis of root diameter in a wild diploid relative of sweetpotato (Ipomoea batatas (L.) Lam.) using a SNP-based genetic linkage map generated by genotyping-by-sequencing
                </a>
              </h3>
